                    LOANAND PROGRAM SUMMARY


Borrower             LebaneseRemblic
ImplementingAgencies MinistryofFinance.
Amount               LoanofUS$lOO million
Terms                Fixed-spreadloan in US$with up to 15 years maturity and 5
                     years graceperiod
Tranching            Singletranche loanto be disburseduponeffectiveness
Description          The proposed Reform Implementation Development Policy
                     Loan (RIDPL) i s in support of the implementationof the Paris
                     I11reformprogrampresentedbythe GovernmentofLebanonto
                     the January 25, 2007 international donor conference and as
                     reflectedinthe InterimStrategyNote for Lebanon(ReportNo.
                     39779-LB).       The Paris I11 reform program comprises
                     comprehensive measures to improve fiscal sustainability,
                     enhance growth, and deepenthe social reforms. The proposed
                     RIDPL supports these objectives with a particularemphasison
                     the energy sector reform, which is a cornerstone ofthe Paris I11
                     reform program. The unreliable energy supply is a major
                     impediment to investment and employment in Lebanon and
                     because ofthe huge energy subsidyburden-equalto US$1per
                     personper day - a severe drain on the Government budget of
                     resources for growth and poverty reduction. In addition,
                     visible movement on such a fundamental area of reform will
                     contribute to the credibility of the Government's overall
                     program and should command broad-based support from key
                     stakeholders. The operation also supports actions inthe areas
                     of businessclimateand social sector reform.
Benefits             The benefits of the proposed operation will be realized over
                     time. Benefits are expected to accrue to the Government
                     budget through reducedsubsidy requirementsto the electricity
                     utility, EdL, thereby freeing up scarce public resources for
                     growthandpoverty reduction. Beneficiarieswill also include
                     industrial and household consumers who will enjoy more
                     reliable electricity supply and reduced reliance on more
                     expensive back-up generation. This, in turn, should lead to
                     positive investment and employment impact from more
                     reliable electricity supply. Reducingimpedimentsto business
                     will enhance Lebanon's competitive position.            Social
                     protection reforms will benefit the poor and vulnerable,
                     particularlyas the economygoes througha difficult adjustment
                     periodoverthe comingyears.
Risks                The main risks under this operation arise from the ongoing
                     Dolitical crisis in Lebanon which could result in a failure to



                                        iv

                   undertake key fiscal and sectoral reforms in a timely fashion.
                   Partly mitigating this risk is the fact that an important part o f
                   the reforms supported under this operation have broad-based
                   support.   In the case of the energy sector, reforms were
                   designed by the former Minister o f Energy and Water, who i s
                   now a member o f the Opposition,             and received full
                   endorsement from the coalition Government at the time (mid-
                   2006).   The reforms are based on the 2002 Electricity Law
                   adopted by a representative Parliament with a strong
                   consensus; they do not require parliamentary approval and can
                   be implemented through Council o f Minister decisions as the
                   highest level. The ongoing early engagement by the Bank in
                   the development and implementation o f a social protection
                   strategy could also help build social consensus around the
                   reform program and provide an additional mitigation
                   instrument. Exposure risks to the Bank beyond the current
                   operation will be controlled through close monitoring o f
                   performance with respect to the Paris I11reform program. As
                   with this operation so also with subsequent operations, the
                   Government will be asked to provide an implementation
                   progress report and Bank staff will make their own assessment
                   as well. If the Paris I11reform program i s implemented in a
                   satisfactory manner, Bank support will continue to help
                   achieve mediumterm macroeconomic and sector objectives.

   INTERNATIONAL BANK FOR RECONSTRUCTIONAND DEVELOPMENT
  PROGRAM DOCUMENT FOR A PROPOSEDREFORMIMPLEMENTATION
                    DEVELOPMENT POLICY LOANTO LEBANON


                                      I. INTRODUCTION

1.       Lebanon's economic and social challenges are tremendous. Since the mid 1990s,
public debt levels have risen steadily to account for over 178% o f GDP in 2005.
Concurrently, the competitiveness o f its enterprises has dwindled and the lack o f
employment opportunities has ledto substantial poverty and emigration.

2.       Conflict and sectarian politics, weak public institutions, lack o f planning, poor
governance and several unimplementedreform programs, along with frequent changes in
government (accompanied by frequent changes in economic and sectoral policies) have
contributed to the challenges that Lebanon faces.                        These challenges have been
exacerbated by the hostilities that broke out between Lebanon and Israel during July-
August 2006, resultinginserious humanandphysical losses and underminingconfidence
inthe Lebanese economy.'

3.       Recognizing that rebuilding confidence in the economy requires reform measures
in key sectors, the Government of Lebanon was quick to finalize its medium-term
structural reform plans and call for an international donor conference to discuss this
program and provide indications o f support for the medium term. The conference was
heldon January 25,2007 inParis (Paris 111) and the program received the endorsement o f
the international donor community, which pledged US$7.6 billion in support over the
medium-term. The pledged assistance (mostly in the form of soft loans) includes both
budgetary support and project-specific finance.

4.       The Paris I11reform program is comprehensive and spans several areas that are
critical for Lebanon's growth, fiscal and social welfare prospects. Key pillars o f the
program are:       (i)fiscal adjustment to reverse Lebanon's presently unfavorable debt
dynamics and reduce the debt-to-GDP ratio through rationalization o f expenditures,
enhancement o f revenues, and improved efficiency; among the key measures are energy
sector reform and privatization (focusing on selected telecommunications assets and
licenses in the initial phase); (ii)      growth enhancing structural reforms to strengthen the
business environment and the competitiveness o f Lebanon's businesses; and (iii)social
sector reform to strengthen social safety nets to reduce poverty and vulnerability and
increase efficiency and effectiveness o f social spendingincluding inhealth and education.
The program also covers rehabilitation and reconstruction following the hostilities,

'The  direct damage from the hostilities is estimated at US$2.8 billion, with indirect damages accounting for
another US$700-US$SOO million in losses. Following the ceasefire, the World Bank was quick to respond
and carried out an Economic and Social ImpactAssessment (ESIA) in close coordination with other donors.
In addition, a US$70 million Trust Fundwas established to assist the Government address a set of critical
investments and reforms, including technical assistance to reform the power sector.


                                                      6

including through the emergency assistance mobilized before and at the Stockholm
Conference on August 31,2006.

5.       At the Paris I11Conference, the World Bankpledgedits assistanceto Lebanon (up
to US$700 million in IBRD financing) based on satisfactory implementation o f key
reforms under the Government's program. Towards this end, an Interim Strategy Note
(ISN) has been prepared because the underlying context has changed dramatically since
the last Country Assistance Strategy (CAS) was discussed by the Board on December 15,
2005.

6.       The I S N is anchored in the Government's medium-term reform program
presented at Paris 111. Despite the current politically charged environment, the reform
momentum has been maintained with the first steps towards implementation o f the
program underway.         Embedded in a forward looking strategy, the I S N outlines a
transitional framework o f technical and financial assistance to contribute to this early
effort. It advances a medium-termpublic expenditure and social reform agenda, with a
particular focus on energy, social protection, and water sector reforms, which are critical
to improve the larger macroeconomic andfiscal outlook over time.

7.       This proposed Reform Implementation Development Policy Loan (RZDPL) i s
integral to the support strategy described inthe ISN. While the RIDPL i s anchored inthe
medium term agenda o f the Paris I11reform program, it has a particular focus on the
energy sector reforms that are likely to be critical to the achievement o f medium-term
macro-fiscal targets set out in the program. The energy sector suffers from years o f
overall poor governance, weak management and administration, highinefficiencies inthe
power infrastructure ranging from power plants to the transmission and distribution
networks and reliance on costly imported fuel for power generation despite long-standing
plans to introduce natural gas.2 The result is unreliable electricity supply, very low
consumer confidence in the electric utility, Electricit6 du Liban (EdL), inadequate cost-
recovery and considerable dependence on budgetarysubsidies as highlightedabove.

8.       The Government is well aware o f the need to turn the sector around. In2002, an
Electricity Law (Law 462) was passed which lays out the restructuring intentions o f the
power sector. The Electricity Law calls for: the creation o f a regulatory authority; the
unbundlingof all or part ofthe sector; the incorporation under commercial law ofthe new
entities; and ultimately the privatization o f up to 40% o f the shares. It i s challenging but
all the more urgent to implement the Electricity Law successfully in Lebanon today and
underthe growing subsidiesto the sector.

9.       Through implementation o f reforms to improve governance and service reliability
and reduce the cost o f production o f electricity, the subsidy requirements could be
reduced from their current very highlevels, amounting to about US$1per person per day

* Power iscurrently producedusingimportedheavyfuel oil and light fbel oil, withthe latter costingalmost
twicethe priceper tonofheavyfbel oil. The light fuel oil is usedin50% ofthe powerplantswhich are
designedto operate on naturalgas.


                                                  7

or about 20% o f annual Government revenues and about 3.3% o f GDP (before debt
service) in 2006.3 The proposed RIDPL supports a set o f actions required to kick start
these reforms as a complementary initial step in the implementation o f the full Paris I11
reform program.         This in turn sends a strong signal to the Lebanese public and
international stakeholders that a credible structural adjustment and fiscal adjustment
program i s underway which should help maintain the reform momentum under current
difficult economic, political and security circumstances.                 The fiscal and structural
adjustment measures, in the energy sector and beyond, are anticipated to be broadened
and deepened as the reform program is phased-inover the medium-term.

                                11.       COUNTRY CONTEXT

A.       RECENT ECONOMIC DEVELOPMENTS INLEBANON4

10.      Inrecent years, Lebanon's economy has been substantially affected by external
events. Between2001 and 2004, the average growth rate reached 4%. In2005, economic
performance deteriorated inthe wake o f the assassination o f former Prime Minister Rafiq
Hariri. 2006 was shaping upto produce the highestgrowthrate ina decade due inpartto
the regional boom stoked by the rise in the price o f oil which have favored demand for
Lebanon's goods and services and induced transfers and capital inflows fi-om the Gulf
countries. Had this performance continued, real GDP growth would have been about 6
percent for the full calendar year. Instead, real GDP growth was flat in 2006 with lost
output from the hostilities reaching about US$1.3 billi~n.~

11.      The hostilities eliminated the good fiscal outcome o f the first six months o f 2006,
though subsequent international support helped contain the negative effects o f the
hostilities and political tensions on foreign reserves. Following a primary surplus over
the preceding five years, the primaryfiscal balance (excluding grants) reversed to a deficit
of 1.3 percent o f GDP; and the overall deficit widened to 14.6 percent o f GDP. Despite
higher budgetarygrants (2.9 percent of GDP) thanthe estimated direct budgetaryimpact
o f the hostilities (about 1 percent o f GDP), the overall fiscal deterioration in2006 reflects
the combined effects o f increase in interest payments and primary expenditures and
decline inrevenues. Although the hostilities playedan important role inboostingprimary
expenditures, persistent deficits runby public agencies (most notably EdL to which total
budgetary transfers rose by 65% in 2006) also played a role. With the increase in the
fiscal deficit and the collapse ineconomic growth, gross public debt remainedhigh at 178
percent o f GDP in2006 against earlier (pre-hostilities) expectations o f a decline. A large
injection o f Saudi (US$1billion) andKuwaiti (US$500 million) deposits duringand after
the hostilities largely contained any adverse effects of the hostilities on gross reserves.
However, with the rise in political tensions since late 2006, gross reserves gradually



 MinistryofFinance,Lebanon,2007, includingdebt service; 4% includingdebtservice.
 This sectiondraws heavilyonthe draft LebanonInterimStrategyNote(June2007).
  Extensive destructionof physical capital and disruption of trade, tourism, markets, and supply channels
duringthe hostilities hammeredall sectors ofthe economy, from agriculture and industryto services.


                                                   8

declined from their end-2006 level o f US$10.2 billion to about US$9.6 billion by end-
May 2007.

12.      The fiscal indicators available so far in 2007 indicate relatively strong revenue
collection performance during the first four months o f the year (9 percent increase
inclusive o f grants) over the same period in2006, when fiscal performance was similarly
strong. However, the increase in expendituresduringthe same period (23 percent) more
than compensated for this improvement, leading to a lower primary surplus (US$330
million) compared to the same period in2006 (US$5 14 million). Increasing debt service
payments (17 percent) andprimary expenditures (27 percent) continue to be the drivers of
the rising expenditures, with about 78 percent o f the increase inthe latter accounted by
transfers to the Electricite duLiban(EdL) alone.6

13.      The ongoing political stalemate and more recently the eruption o f the security
crisis7 dampens the confidence boosting impact o f the comprehensive economic reform
program and the generous Paris I11financial package that marked the beginningo f 2007.
Currently, the political situation in Lebanon i s tense, with the Opposition block calling
for greater representation inthe Government, and the Speaker o f the House declining to
convene the Parliament. Six Cabinet Ministers resigned inNovember 2006 and the anti-
Government demonstrations infront o f Government headquarters have paralyzed parts o f
Beirut for months. Talks are underway, including through international mediation, to
seek a solutionthat satisfies the political parties. Presidential election i s scheduled for the
Fall o f 2007.

B.       MACROECONOMIC               OUTLOOK AND DEBTSUSTAINABILITY~


14.      Lebanon's macro economic outlook i s presented in the I S N for Lebanon. The
macro economic and fiscal analysis inthe I S Nbuilds on the Government's medium-term
reform program and i s aligned with the assessment undertaken as part o f the IMF's
recently approved EmergencyPost Conflict Arrangement (EPCA) Loan (see more under
section IV, B).

15.      The short-term outlook presented in the I S N takes into account the current
political impasse inLebanon and i s conservative. During 2007, real GDP i s expected to
grow by only 1percent and key sustainability indicators, such as the primary balance and
the debt ratio, will not improve by much (see Table 1). These considerations are
consistent with the Government's implementation plan whereby most o f the reform

17.      Structural reforms that would improve the business environment, competition, the
entry and operation o f SMEs and service delivery would upgrade, to some degree, the
long-term growth potential o f the Lebanese economy. Many of these reforms (including
telecom privatization and energy sector reform) are considered urgent by private sector

  The agenda for 2007 includes a commitment to contain the deficit and initiate important structural
reforms, including steps toward privatization o f the two mobile businesses and energy sector reforms,

reforms.

representatives and will raise business confidence. This would help stimulate growth and
reduce interest rate spreads, thereby helpingoffset the negative short term impact of fiscal
adjustment on aggregate demand.

by reducingthe deficit o fthe sector to 1.5% o f GDP by 2012 (from 3.3% in2006). Table

21.      The social protection reforms will benefit the poor and vulnerable in Lebanon,
particularly as the economy goes through a difficult adjustment period over the period
ahead, and contribute to fiscal improvement in the medium term. These reforms are
comprehensive ranging from expanding social insurance (pensions and health insurance)
by reforming the National Social Security Fund (NSSF) to strengthening the reach of
social safety nets and public health services. At the center o f the social safety net reform
i s the creation o f a targeting mechanism for social assistance which will strengthen the
poverty and vulnerability focus o f the social safety net programs. Both some o f the poor


                                                   14

and the middle class will also benefit from the social insurance reforms through the
introduction o f a pension system and more efficient and effective health insurance. The
NSSF reforms will also gradually eliminate Government's implicit liabilities (e.g. from
83 percent o f GDPto 52 percent o f GDP already in2015).

22.     The macroeconomic assessment presented above allows no further space for
slippages in implementation o f the reform program over the medium-term. It assumes
that ongoing technical assistance and capacity building efforts along with initial reform
measures planned this year would pave the way for sustained implementation o f reforms
from 2008 onwards. Delays or lack o f progress in the implementation o f the reform
program would be very costly for Lebanon. As shown in Table 1, even with significant
fiscal adjustment, large donor financing, and fairly positive growth response in the
medium term, debt will remain high (135 percent o f GDP in 2010) and require strong
continued effort to be brought to sustainable levels.     Should there be a weak policy
response or postponement o f reforms more generally; the debt-to-GDP level could reach
over 200% o f GDP over the medium-termaccording to the debt sustainability analysis
undertakenas part o f the Bank's ESIA. This would inevitably further expose the country
to a risk o f financial disruption.

                 111.     THE GOVERNMENT'SREFORMPROGRAM

23.     The main objective of the Government's reform program is to stimulate growth,
create employment, reduce poverty and maintain social and political stability.         The
program i s an integrated package covering three main pillars described below. Annex 1
providesa Letter o f Development Policy (LDP) from the Government and a report on the
implementation progress o fthe reforms so far.

A.      Fiscal Adiustment and Structural Reforms

24.     Revenue Management is an important element o f this pillar and encompasses both
improved revenue administration and enhanced collection through greater automation,
unification o f tax procedures, implementationo f a General Income Tax (GIT), increases
in VAT and interest tax rates, and adjustment o f the gasoline excise rate back to pre-
capping levels, and improved revenue collection from public properties.

25.     Prior to the partial privatization of the electricity sector, the Government
envisages a Privatization Program, focusing on the sale o f the mobile licenses and the
partial sale o f Liban Telecom (the fixed line operator, which will also include an
additional and third mobile license to operate in Lebanon). The proceeds from these
transactions are expected to be significant and are very important for Lebanon's fiscal and
debt sustainability. Includedunder the program is also preparation o fthe privatization o f
other sectors such water and wastewater, ports as well as potentially the existing but non-
operational oil refineries.




                                            15

26.     Further fiscal consolidation is expected through measures related to Primaw
Expenditures, notably public wages, which will be contained through closing o f various
funds and automation of administrative procedures to reduce the need for man-power in
addition to an overall review and identification o f existing excess staffing in the public
sector.

27.     The primary expenditure measures respond to a most challenging issue in
Lebanon today, namely, the need for expenditure containment through reform qf the
Enerav Sector which is a highpriority given the sector's substantial claim on budgetary
resources. The reforms will support the corporatization o f a restructuredEdL, a reduction
intechnical losses and illegal consumption of electricity, introduction of natural gas for
power generation and the completion o f on-going high priority investments as well as
attracting the private sector to undertake new investmentrequirementsto allow for better
service provision. These measures are envisaged to lead to a sustained reduction in the
budgetary contributions made by the Government to the electricity utility over time,
thereby contributing to fiscal adjustment.       Eventually - and as service improves -
electricity tariffs will also need to be revisedto better reflect the cost o f service provision.

28.     In addition, a Public Investment Program with increased share of foreign
financing (target 45% o f total investments) will be adopted.           Reforms will also be
implementedwith regardto Expnditure Management. The reforms will focus on a new
and improved Budget System Law with increased coverage in the budget, including
coverage o f foreign-financed investments, a medium term expenditure framework,
limitation o f budget carry-overs in current expenditures and more disciplined treasury
advances and a single account for the Treasury to improvecash management.

B.      Growth EnhancingStructural Reforms

29.     Underthis pillar, the Government will implementmeasuresaimed at (i)            improved
governance through auditing o f public finances and enterprises, new procurement laws
and regulation, renewedrole o f the Courts o f Accounts to ensure budget spendingalong
budgetary allocations, and better controls to monitor operations in the public sector; (ii)
strengthened capital markets and debt management through introduction o f laws,
establishment o f a Capital Market Commission and specialized Financial Court and
introduction o f new instruments to further market liquidity; and (iii)      improved business
environment through membershipinthe World Trade Organization (WTO), streamlining
o f procedures to establish and manage businesses including simplified import/export
administrative procedures, facilitated trade, enhanced access to credit, and improved
competitiveness and governance o f firms..

C.      Social Sector Reforms

30.     The aim o f the social sector reforms is to strengthen social safety nets to reduce
poverty and vulnerability, increase efficiency and effectiveness o f social spending,
enhance delivery of social programs, reform health and education sectors andthe pension



                                               16

system. Key to strengtheningthe poverty focus o f Social Safetv Nets is the introduction
o f an appropriate targeting mechanism. Reducing duplication between ministries i s also
on top o f the agenda as i s revising service standards for contracts with non-public
providers,   In Education, the Government will strengthen planning and policy to
rationalize the operating budgets o f the sector while targeting the specific problems o f
drop-outs and enrollment among the poor. Inthe Health sector, service will be improved
through better targeting and increasing availability o f vouchers for eligible patients while
introducing evaluationtechniques to ensure increased efficiency of spendingand service.

31.     Another key reform that the Government is introducing under this pillar i s
Pension Reform aiming at introduction o f a fully-funded defined contribution pension
system to replace the current End-of-Service-Indemnity(EOSI) program and stop the
accumulation o f unfunded liabilities. The past two consecutive governments have been
in agreement with this reform and technical discussions on a draft law reforming the
EOSI and introducing a fully-funded defined contributions pension system has been
completed by a specialized parliamentary committee. Implementationo f the new system
would focus on the drafting o f necessary regulations (e.g. treatment o f annuities,
survivorship pensions, and disability pensions; governance o f the new Social Security
InvestmentAuthority).

         IV.     BANKSUPPORT TO THE GOVERNMENT'S STRATEGY

A.      LINKTO CAS/ISN


32.     The latest CAS was approved by the Board o f Directors on December 15 2005,
covering the 2006-2009 period. The CAS focused on three pillars: (i)         governance for
economic management and growth support; (ii)    the development ofhumancapital andthe
mitigation o f the poverty effects o f transition; and (iii)resource and environmental
management. An Interim Strategy has become necessary because o f the hostilities inthe
summer o f 2006, and the domestic political impasse that has prevailed since November,
2006 which have fundamentally changed the underlying economic and political context,
rendering certain CAS assumptions, projections and triggers for Bank assistance
untenable.

33.     The InterimStrategy provides a framework for the continuation o f the Bank's role
as a development partner for Lebanon as it moves forward from its current difficult
economic circumstances. The period covered by the Interim Strategy (12-15 months) i s
short inappreciation o f the uncertain economic and political situation inLebanon andthe
forthcoming elections in the Fall. Embedded in a forward looking strategy around the
same three pillars that characterized the last full CAS, the Interim Strategy sets a
transitional framework of technical and financial assistance to contribute to the
Government's efforts to establish reform momentum. It advances a medium-termpublic
expenditure and social reform agenda, with a particular focus on energy, social
protection, and water sector reforms. It i s widely understood in Lebanon that upfront
quick reform action in these areas and supporting international assistance are essential



                                           17

for: (i)contributing to fiscal sustainability in the medium term; (ii)supporting the
Government's growth and social reform agenda; and (iii)       catalyzing donors to implement
their Paris I11pledgesto achieve highimpact reforms that would put Lebanonon a track
to macroeconomic stability and growth.

B.       COLLABORATIONWITH THE IMFAND OTHER DONORS


34.      The IMF approved an Emergency Post Conflict Assistance (EPCA) program for
SDR50.75 million (US$77 million) on April 9, 2007. The EPCA has been disbursed
against a set o f fiscal and structural policy reforms to be undertaken during2007 aimedat
protecting financial stability and containing the budget deficit during this transition year
andinitiating structural reforms that are critical to the success o fthe medium-termreform
program. Monitorable actions under the EPCA package involve actions in the areas o f
tax reforms, budgetarymanagement improvement, telecom privatization, and preparation
o f audits o fthe National Social Security Fundandthe power sector. The Government has
indicatedthat upon successful completion o f the EPCA, it intends to seek further support
for its program through a stand-by arrangementfrom the Fund.

35.      In the wake of the hostilities, IFC directed its support to the recovery of the
private sector. At the Paris I11conference, IFC pledgedUS$250-275 million to this for a
program o f investment and technical assistance. A series o f credit lines and risk sharing
facilities with local banks for US$200 million will support small and medium enterprises
in their recovery.      Additionally, IFC has expanded its Trade Finance program and
continues to be active in both real sector and services sector investments. Advisory
services to the Government also form a vital component o f IFC's assistance. The IFC has
been asked to provide advisory support to solicit an IndependentPower Producer (IPP)
andpossibly other steps inthe privatization o f some existingpower plants. Finally, FIAS
and IFC have been assisting with design and implementation o f administrative reform,
and a number o f initiatives are underway to increase accessto finance.

36.      The donor community has been generous in supporting the reconstruction and
recovery o f Lebanon, pledgingUS$7.6 billion at the Paris I11conference inJanuary 2007.
The spectrum o f donors extends from Europe to the USA and many generous Middle
Eastern donors. Highincome countries from the Gulf Cooperation Council (UnitedArab
Emirates, Kuwait, Qatar, and Saudi Arabia) have been particularly instrumental in
supporting the social sector inthe areas o f emergency relief and reconstruction; currently
the Bank participates very actively in energy and social protection reform. Meanwhile,
USAID, EIB, and many regional donors (AFESD, KFAED, IsDB), remain major
contributors inthe areas o f governance, economic reform and growth.

37.      Support to the power sector has beenequally diverse, with the major players being
AFD, EC, EIB and AFSED. The AFD and the Bank have been inclose cooperation in
the preparation o f the Bank's emergency response under the LebanonTrust Fundfacility.
AFD is contributing to the cost o f the technical assistance under the Emergency Power
Sector Reform Capacity Reinforcement Project and has been instrumental in securing


                                                18

grant financing for the Master Plan, for which consultant engagement is a condition under
the proposed RIDPL. The EC and EIB have both expressed support to the reform of the
energy sector; in particular EIB has earmarked funds for advancing the PPP agenda
through technical assistance and guarantee instruments.          The Government is also
discussing with the Saudi Fund, Kuwait Fund and the Arab Fund for Economic and
Social Developmenttheir possible involvement inthe energy sector.

38.     The Government continues to coordinate with donors to arrange for the most
suitable allocation o f their pledged funds with an emphasis on raising additional budget
support for reforms, or making sure that funds for physical investmentsare aligned with
the Government's reform agenda andexpenditure framework.

C.      RELATIONSHIPTO OTHER BANK OPERATIONS


39.     The portfolio of projects active before the hostilities includes activities in the
urban, transport, water, education, cultural heritage and social protection sectors. A Trust
Fund for Lebanon, established with US$70 million from IBRD surplus in September
2006, is supporting municipal reconstruction in some o f the worst affected communities
and assisting inthe rehabilitationo fwater networksinthe WesternBeka'a. Additionally,
technical assistance vital to the reform program i s beingprovided inthe water, energy and
social protection sectors.

40.     At present, the main social protection operation is the Community Development
Project (CDP), which was recently restructured. It seeks to improve the living conditions
and economic status o f disadvantaged communities in Lebanon.              An Institutional
Development Grant (IDF) supports the development o f HIV/AIDS monitoring systems
and public health programs. Additionally, a Rapid Social Assessment was undertaken
post-hostilities to identifynewpockets o fvulnerability.

41.     In the energy sector, a TFL grant of US$5 million has been allocated to a
Technical Assistance (TA) project, namely "Emergency Power Sector Reform Capacity
Reinforcement Project", to initiate reforms and restructuring o f the electricity sector.
More specifically, the project supports: (i)the Ministry o f Energy and Water (MEOW) to
address key sector policy issues and high priority cost reduction projects (such as better
management o fthe distribution network and fuel supply and procurement); (ii) Higher
                                                                                 the
Council o f Privatization (HCP) to corporatize EdL; and (iii) itself to improve its
                                                                  EdL
operations and financial performance.

42.     While it is evident that current Bank support is small relative to the needs of
Lebanon, the analytic and advisory services constitute an integral and catalytic parts o f
overall donor support, which is predicated on reforms, particularly in the energy and
social protection sectors.




                                             19

D.    LESSONSLEARNED

43.   There are several important lessons learned from attempting policy reforms and
implementingoperations infragile anduncertainpolicy environments. These lessons are
drawn from the previous Lebanon CAS and the Bank's broader experience with policy-
based operations.

   rn Fully appreciating thepolitical complexities and thepolitical economy dfficulties
      of the decision-making environment in Lebanon. The system o f governance in
      Lebanon poses non-trivial challenges to implementing policy reforms in the
      country. Any attempt at policy reforms must be done with a true appreciation o f
      the impliedpolitical complexities for designand implementation.

   rn Engaging in lending operations when a credible and broad-based policy
      commitment isfully ascertained. The implementationtrack record o fthe portfolio
      inLebanonindicates the strong needto secure upfront political commitment prior
      to engaging in a new lendingoperation, including getting buy-infrom members o f
      Parliament early on in the preparation. The power sector program i s anchored in
      Law 462 passed by Parliament in 2002 and the reform plan proposed by the
      former Minister o fEnergyand Water inJuly 2006.

   rn Supporting the reform program through appropriate instruments, e.g. budget
      assistance rather than investment has more impact in facilitating Lebanons
      reform program at this stage. In this context, given the weak management
      capacity o fthe electricity sector, it would not be conducive to focus on large scale
      infrastructure projects investments until the management of the sector i s
      improved. Additionally, budget support assistance provides the convening power
      for the Government to address key sector bottlenecks.

   rn The Bank's experience with DPLs in other j?agile/post-conflict states indicates
      that in states that show slow improvements due to afragile political environment
      policy-based lending can provide a much needed sense of policy direction,
      allowing governments to look beyond immediate problems and begin addressing
      key structural development constraints. This "continuity in policy at a time of
      change" is applicable for the prevailing conditions o f Lebanon.

   rn It is important to keep an eye on short-term Jiscal trends, but a deep
      understanding of      structural    and institutional constraints to       long-term
      development should also be developed.

   rn Speed and long-term engagement are needed in post conflict situations. The
      speed o f the Bank's response in the aftermath o f the hostilities, effective field
      presence and detailed knowledge o f country circumstances were especially
      important to enable strong dialogue and provide valued support on the ground to



                                             20

         respond to priority needs emerging from the hostilities and to guide wider Bank
         engagement.

E.       ANALYTICAL UNDERPINNINGS


44.      The analytical underpinningso f the proposed operation are covered by multiple
 studies conducted in recent years. Among them are a Sources of Growth Study, an
Investment Climate Assessment, a Public Expenditure Review, and a Financial
Accountability Assessment. There is a also a substantial body o fanalytical work on social
 sectors covering the areas o f social insurance (health financinghnsurance and pension
reform), social safety nets and public health. The gist o f these studies i s captured in the
Economic and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) report that was undertaken following the
 summer-2006 hostilities.      The ESIA covered sixteen sectors in three broad areas
 (infrastructure,  social, and economic) assessing the macroeconomic and sector
 development challenges, the complexities added by the physical, economic and social
impact o f the hostilities and outlining sector reform priorities. The Government's reform
program was informed inpart bythe findings and recommendations o fthe ESIA. Finally,
 a portion o f the Trust Fundfor Lebanon (TFL) has been directed for advisory assistance
for reinvigorating the private sector and for capacity building and/or implementation
 support activities in energy and social protection sectors, which provides analytical
feedback inthese areas.

45.      Concerning the energy sector, the most recent analysis is covered in the ESIA
which includes a chapter on electricity, informing the Government's reform program in
the sector and providing recommendations in the short- and medium-term. The key
 strategic points are (i) major efforts are needed to improve governance; (ii)        improved
 commercial operation o f EdL requires immediate change in its legal status through
 corporatization to remove staffing and managerial problems but also to reduce the day to
 day political interference; (iii)
                                 all efforts should be put towards accessing natural gas; and
 (iv) the significant investment needs of the sector can only be economic and efficient if
there i s better management and skills at EdL and there i s proper sector strategy and
planning.

46.      Furthermore, in 2004, the World Bank carried out a Hydrocarbon Strategy
 Study." The objective o f the study was to evaluate the best market structure for the
supply and transport of natural gas inLebanon. While the report clearly evaluated very
positively the introduction o f natural gas to Lebanon in terms o f foreign exchange, trade
balance, financial viability o f the electricity sector as well as on the environment, it
stressed as a first priority the need to restructure EdL and to put it under private
management (and ultimately ownership) to enable the conversion o f fuel in the sector.
Without a restructured EdL, the potential achievements o f fuel conversion will be
significantly undermineddue to current lack o f capacity, incentives and skills.



~~~




"  Republic o fLebanon: HydrocarbonStrategy Study, June 2004. The World Bank: Report29579-LE.


                                               21

47.     Around the same time, BNP Paribas prepared a privatization strategy.I2 Intheir
presentation they stated that they believed that investors would be willing to consider
purchasing generation and distribution assets inLebanonprovided certain elements are in
place: (i)  guarantees covering regulatory and public entities' payment risk; (ii) lessthe
attractive distribution areas (North, South and BekaaValley) be eliminated from the sales
offer; (iii)non strategic investors, including local, be involved; (iv) protection against the
currency devaluation risk by means o f a pass through o f such risk to consumers via an
appropriate tariff formula be agreed to; and (v) the investor i s provided an adequate
period o f exclusivity in generation and supply. Inthe absence o f these elements, Paribas
believedthat investors/operators would probably not be willing to purchase assets and the
Government would need to consider alternatives such as concessions, O&M or
management contracts.

48.     As part of the privatization work, National Economic Research Associates
(NERA) undertook a reviewo ftariffs. Their findingswere that before privatization some
actions should be envisaged, such as increasing revenues either by raising the average
level o f tariffs or by reducing non-technical losses; removing cross subsidies (major
cross-subsidies are between low voltage and high voltage customers and between
concessions and everyone else); some tariff adjustments would be required to achieve
cost recovery on privatization. Since the study was completed, the gap between cost and
revenues has widened considerably, along with an even greater deteriorationinthe supply
o f electricity due to increased theft and reduced routine maintenance o f equipment. As
such, tariff restructuring and increase i s a key requirement to improve the financial
viability o f the sector over time, but remains socially and politically unfeasible in
Lebanon today. Hence, revenue enhancement will have to come, in the shorter term,
from loss reduction, lower cost generation o f electricity through fuel conversion and fiom
improvements inbilling and collection.

49.     The Bank has also undertakenwork on the cost o f environmental degradation in
Lebanon (in2000). Expressed in GDP per year, the cost was estimated between 2.8% to
4.0%, with a mean estimate o f close to US$565 million per year, or 3.4% o f GDP. The
second largest impact after water was reported to derive from air pollution (1.1%). This
includes the impact o f fuel used at power stations (such as Zouk and Jieh). A specific
study on the cost of environmental degradation being carried out at present and relates to
the July 2006 hostilities. This review will focus mainly on the impact o f the oil spill,
ruble removal, water quality and forest fires.

50.     The Bank is currently preparing an energy sector PER. The PER is reviewingthe
expenditures o f the electricity sector; and i s assessing the cost-benefit o f expenditures in
relation to their contribution to a reduction in the claim on Government resources. The
PER is also reviewingthe operating expenditures of the sector, with a view to provide
recommendations for how to contain the expenditures.




l2EDLPrivatization, Strategy Report, September2002. BNP Paribas.


                                               22

                         V.      THE PROPOSEDOPERATION

A.      OPERATIONDESCRIPTION


51.     The proposed operation is the Bank's response to the Paris I11reform program.
As described in the sections above, Paris I11covers several important reforms aimed at
getting Lebanon back on track in terms o f macroeconomic and fiscal sustainability,
underpinnedby social reforms that will enable the implementationo f economic and fiscal
reforms.   The key reforms in the Government's program cover (i) enhancing
                                                                         growth
reforms which will be achieved through reform and privatization o f state-owned
enterprises (energy, telecommunications), and an improvement in the overall business
environment enabling more businesses to establish at lower transaction costs; (ii)   fiscal
reforms which will be achieved through tax policy adjustments, and savings from a
reduction in the subsidy transfers to EdL as a result o f implemented energy reforms and
increase in the efficiency o f expenditures; and (iii)social sector reforms to increase the
efficiency o f social service provision through consolidation o f institutions and better
targeted utilization o f resources and ensure those most vulnerable and impacted by the
overall reform program are protected. The third area i s critical to mitigate and therefore
enable the implementation o fthe growth enhancing and fiscal reforms.

52.     The overall development objective o fthe RIDPL program is to support a select set
o f reforms under the Government's program where (i)strong consensus exists; (ii)       the
Bank has deep knowledge and has assumed a very active role; and (iii)            reform i s
sufficiently advanced to justify development policy lending. To this end, the RIDPL
focuses on the areas that are at the center o f the Government's reform program: (i)
energy sector reform; (ii)modernization o f the business environment; and (iii)      social
protection reform.     As such, the operation will support, as condition for Board
presentation, a distinct set o f reforms in the energy sector, business environment, social
safety nets and social insurance. Two public financial management measures are also
conditions for Board presentation (see Annex 2 for presentation o f key actions in matrix
form).

53.     These key areas are consistent with the Governance, Economic Management, and
Growth Support as well as the Developing Human Capital and Mitigating the Poverty
Effects o f Transition pillars o f the ISN. The RIDPL i s a single tranche operation. The
ISNenvisages a second RIDPL, subject to satisfaction of conditions for this, which will
provide continuity in the Bank's assistance for broadening and deepening the
Government's reform program. The single tranche DPL approach under the ISN allows
for quick implementation o f some initial reforms, while providing both the Bank and the
Government with flexibility to adjust to changing circumstances with respect to the next
phase.

54.     Although initial reform steps are modest, they will be advanced inthe short term
through the technical and financial assistance envisaged both by the Bank and other


                                             23

donors. The proposed combination o f reforms will, inturn, increase the chances for the
Government's comprehensive reform program to gain momentum and be phased-in over
time. It is highly unlikely that the wide-ranging, deep structural reforms of the type that
Lebanon needs to undertake can be implemented without first reversingthe fiscal drain
and service decline in energy sector, reducing impedimentsto business and adequately
addressing the currently underdeveloped social protection agenda.

B.      POLICY AREAS


Reform of the Energy Sector

55.     This RIDPL puts a particular emphasis on initiating administrative reform of the
state-owned enterprise EdL (focusing on improving its capacity and accountability) and
highpriority actions (lower costs, better service) that will contribute to fiscal adjustment
inLebanonandpave the way for deepeningthe reforms over time. This is complemented
bykey public financial management measures (see Section C for details).

56.     The Government's reform program in the energy sector has as the ultimate
objectives to achieve service reliability, cost reduction and relieving the Government
budget o f a massive drain o f resources that could be much better allocated elsewhere.
The Government also aims at reducing the role o f the public sector to ensure improved
conditions for economic growth. The reform steps center on administrative reform o f the
state-owned enterprise EdL and undertaking o f high priority investments to reduce cost
and improve service.      The administrative reform will focus on corporatizing and
unbundlingEdL (generation, transmission and distribution) and ultimately privatizing it.
The priority investmentswill first and foremost be on access to natural gas to replace the
much costlier gas-oil used in about 50% of the current production capacity. This will
initially be by piped natural gas from Egypt to the Beddawi power plant but the
Government i s also investigatingthe option o f liquefied natural gas (LNG) to the Zahrani
power plant.     Furthermore, on-going investments to improve load flow in the
transmission segment will be completed, and upgrading and rehabilitation, including
piloting o f new remote meters in the distribution segment to address non-payment and
technical losses in the network, will be implemented. The Government is investigating
alternatives to privatize the management o f power distribution and has retained
consulting services to manage the tendering process. To the extent possible, financing
will be sought from private sources. Private participation is also being considered to
finance and construct new power generation to address shortage in capacity, and to
improve management o fcurrent facilities.

57.     The Government, through the Higher Council for Privatization, i s also about to
enter into an agreement with the IFC to issue a tender process to attract independent
power producers (IPPs) to invest in the generation o f 450 MW in the Beddawi complex
and possibly acquire the existing Beddawi power plant.




                                            24

 58.     The reform program has been designed based on the achievement of reforms
 undertakenin the last twelve months which include: (i)       appointment o f Auditors for the
 2001 accounts of EdL; (ii) o f contract for the establishment o fthe National Control
                               award
 Center (NCC) which will allow for better dispatch o f electricity; (iii)      award of contract to
 preparetender for private sector participation in distribution (network O&M, billing and
 collection, and installation o f meters); (iv) award o f contract for private operation and
 maintenance o f the most recently installed power plants (Beddawi and Zahrani); and (v)
 Parliament approval o f amendmentto the Electricity Law to allow for IPPs.

 59.     The Government's strategy for the sector i s captured inthe Letter o fDevelopment
 Policy attached inAnnex 1.

 Electricitvtariffs

 60.     As mentioned above in section IV, E, a very important measure for sector
 improvement and successful implementation o f a private sector-led reform effort, i s the
 restructuring, and ultimately adjustment, o f the electricity tariff. The overall average
 tariff for 2006, based on billed energy, was about US$ cents 9.4 kWh. From a cost
 recovery perspective, the overall average tariff i s low as i s it i s set to cover a fuel price o f
 US$25/barrelwith the current cost per barrelaround US$60/barrel.

 61.     Therefore, it i s evident that the tariff needs to be revised to better reflect cost
 (particularly fuel costs). However, also the tariff structure needs to be revised. The
 tranches o f tariffs for the residential consumers are too wide, andthe invertedblock tariff
+should really peak at about 300 kWh, as i s the case inmost countries, instead o f 500 kWh
 as is the case inLebanon where consumers with a consumption o f electricity inexcess o f
 300 kWh may actually not qualify as economically vulnerable. Furthermore, the peak
 tariff for industryactually encourages self generationand thereby reduces EdL's revenue
 base (this i s important since these are generally paying customers). Finally, the tariff to
 existingdistribution concessions should also be reviewedas it appears too generous..

 62.     Under the EmergencyPower Sector Reform Capacity Reinforcement Project, the
 NERA tariff study will be updated (this is included as part of the terms o f reference for
 the TA consulting assignment to the MOEW). This work will help guide future tariff
 adjustments, which will be considered in tandem with service improvements and a
 reduction in the interruption of supply. Worldwide experience shows that any tariff
 increase when service i s weak and bill collection poor to begin with usually results in a
 further deterioration inpayments andanincrease intheft.

 EnergyActions SupportedUnder the RIDPL

 and follow the recently taken reforms to achieve the underlying goals o f the
 Government's energy sector strategy articulated above.               The actions cover sector
 governance, restructuring and cost reduction, and will contribute- over time - to reduced

Government subsidy requirements. The operation has been designed based on the
Government's reform program as presented to Paris I11 and as presented in the
Government's Letter o f Development Policy attached in Annex 1 and seals the critical
steps needed to achieve the objectives o f the energy reform program. The reforms also
reflect the current political context in Lebanon where changes in the composition o f
Government, and in the relations between President, Government, and Parliament, may
occur inthe short-term.

64.      It is recognized that the proposed operation supports what appear to be very
modest reforms due to their institutional nature. However, it needs to be notedthat inthe
context o f Lebanon, the reforms are not modest, and more importantly, they represent the
required sequential steps in the Government's reform program for the sector, e.g., the
necessary governance steps requiredto restructure and reform the sector and accelerate
cost reduction measures through better planning and securing o f alternative fuel sources,
e.g., natural gas. Any follow-on operation(s) would see the restructuringprocess through
to completion andintroduce private sector participation into the sector.

Modernizationof the BusinessClimate

66.    Modernization o f the business climate is o f critical importance to achieve the
Government's economic growth goals. The reforms focus on reducing the impediments
to business that drive up the cost o f doing business locally and act as a barrier for a
modern services-driven economy.

67.     The reform process addresses three key areas (i)reduced transaction costs for
establishing businesses; (ii)better internet and telecom services; and (iii) of labor
                                                                          reform
and social security regulations to generate growth and employment.

68.     To reduce the transaction costs for establishing businesses, the Government, in
cooperation with the IFC,has prepared a two-pronged strategy and implementation plan.
The first prong envisages a major improvement in the business start-up complexity
(measured by procedures, time, and cost) in the short term through streamlining o f
processes and procedures that do not require legislative changes.            The overall
improvement in the complexity o f business-start-ups under the first prong reforms i s
estimated to be around 45 percent compared to current levels. The short-term measures
are now being finalized for implementation, which i s expected to start no later than
September2007, under a Memorandum o fUnderstandingbetweenLibanPost, Ministryo f
Justice, Ministry o f Economy and Trade, Ministry o f Finance, Commercial Registries,
National Social Security Fund, and the Prime Minister's office.             These initial
improvements will be strengthened through reforms (covering initial capital requirements
and further streamlining of processes and procedures) under the second prong o f the
strategy, requiring modifications to the commercial law, and therefore to be implemented
in the medium-term. The overall improvement in the complexity of business-start-ups
under the second prong reforms is estimated to be around 85 percent compared to current
levels.

69.     The telecommunications sector, currently operated as a Government-run
monopoly, acts as a serious barrier to modernizing the economy with poor service inboth
mobile phone and internet services.         This structure impedes the development o f
Information Technology and the creative services sectors, as well as e-commerce, where
Lebanon should have considerable strength.           The Government's reform program
envisages a regulatory system that provides competitive incentives to develop a new
information technology-based industry and telecom privatization, the latter also
contributing to fiscal adjustment through debt reduction.

70.     Important reforms have already beenachieved or are close to being achieved. The
Board o f the Telecom Regulatory Authority (TRA) i s already inplace and its budget for




                                             27

2007 approved.l4 All telecommunications regulatory functions are planned to be
transferred to the TRA by the last quarter o f 2007.         In addition, the corporatization
process o f Liban Telecom and the privatization o f mobile telecommunications are
expected to be launched by end-September 2007, with a view to close transactions in
early 2008.     To this end, submission o f legislation to Parliament by end-June i s a
monitorable action under the IMF's EPCA. Finally, the Ministryo f Telecommunications
launchedbroadband services inthe Beirut area inMay 2007.

71.     Reform o f Lebanon's rigid labor and social security regulations.              Outdated
regulations and systems depress employment and make Lebanese businesses less
competitive. Analysis conducted by the World Bank prior to the hostilities indicatedthat
inabsenceo fthese restrictions, formal sector firms would, onaverage, employ 15percent
more workers. The Government's ongoing efforts, with assistance from the Bank, to
strengthensocial safety nets and reform the pension system would help in lessening the
rigidities o f the labor market, while providing support to the unemployed (by removing
this burden from businesses) and protectionto the vulnerable elderly (see below section
on Social Protection Reform for the reform progress to be supportedwhich will support
this areao fimproving the overall business environment.

Social ProtectionReform

72.     The social sector inLebanon has several shortcomings which result ininefficient
spending in the sector as well as inadequate ability to serve as an effective protection
mechanism.       The shortcomings are particularly prominent in the areas of:             health
insurance, pension systems and health and education services. Given its exposure to
shocks, Lebanon should have much stronger and expanded risk mitigating (insurance)
and risk coping (assistance) mechanisms including health insurance, a pension system,
and a well-targeted cash assistance program. Unique to the region, Lebanon does not
have a pension system for private workers (what exists inan End-of-Service-Indemnity).
While it has a health insurance system, it i s fragmented, costly and formally reaches only
50% o f the population. Formal safety nets are small, scattered, and not targeted to the
poor and vulnerable.     The country relies on its strong confessional-based network o f
social services which play the role o f a safety net (NGOs, welfare organizations,
remittances, etc.) but which are susceptible to economic or political shocks themselves.



l4 The TRA has issued three regulations to date including guidelines on Significant Market Power,
InterconnectionandConsumer Affairs.


                                              28

73.      The Government fully recognizes the problems in the social sectors, and its
reform is a key pillar under the Paris I11reform program. Leading up to Paris 111, several
important steps had beentaken to initiate the reforms, these include: (i) development o f a
Social Action Plan (SAP) in May/June 2006 as well as individual sector strategies; (ii)
creation o f an Inter-Ministerial Committee for Social Policy (IMC-SP);           and (iii)
completion o f technical discussions on a draft pension law by a specialized parliamentary
committee representing the different political sides. The draft law, once approved by
Parliament, will provide Lebanonwith a fully-fundeddefined contribution scheme.

74.      At the request of the Government, the World Bank has assumed a leadingrole in
the designandimplementation ofthe social protectionreform program. These efforts are
supported by an allocation o f funds from the TFL and it i s anticipated that the proposed
second DPL envisaged under the I S N includes Bank support for deeper social reforms.
Duringthe supervision of the proposed RIDPL, reform progress will be monitoredinthe
following key areas:

(i) Reform of the NSSF to improve its financial sustainability, contain the
accumulation o f unfundedliabilities and reduce government transfers. The NSSF today
has unfunded liabilities that could amount to 15 percent o f GDP. One o f the main
objectives i s to restore the financial balance o f the NSSF and reduce the demands for
fiscal support by reforming its health and family allowances branches including
redefining the package o f health services, changing financing mechanisms, and
redesigningcontracting, payment and utilization review systems in order to control costs
and improve the quality o f services. Over the short term, the key measure i s to control
hospitalization costs. For the family allowances branch, the goal i s to target benefits to
middle and low-income families.         Finally, the reform program also includes the
implementation o f the new DC-FF pension system and to refinance its unfunded
liabilities. The new pension system will improve the fiscal position o f the government
(by reducing implicit liabilities) and contribute to reduce the tax-wedge, thus facilitating
employment creation inthe formal sector. The new pension system also has the potential
to support financial sector development, provide alternative sources o f financing to the
corporate sector and thus contribute to productivity and economic growth.

(ii) Reformofsocialsafetynetstostrengthentheirpovertyfocus, buildinstitutional
capacity in the Ministry o f Social Affairs (MOSA), and improve social service delivery
arrangements o f MOSA. Strengtheningthe poverty focus o f the safety net programs will
be achieved through the introduction o f indicator based targeting, or proxy-means testing
(PMT) targeting mechanism. The PMT mechanism will allow social assistance to be
allocated to the vulnerable and poor segments o f the population to smooth income, and
will include compensating those affected by possible public service price changes,
education benefits for the poor, and health subsidiesto those inneed. Implementationo f
the PMT mechanism will take 18-24 months but work has begun on developing the
targeting formula. By September 2007, the formula i s expected to have Government
approval and implementation can commence. At the same time, MOSA's mandate and



                                             29

responsibilities will be redefinedto identify areas o f duplication betweenMOSA, M O H
and MEHE with a view to reduce any duplication and therefore inefficiencies and
wasteful spending. A time-bound action plan reducing duplication between the three
social ministries would be approved by the Social IMC. Finally, with respect to service
delivery through NGOs,these will be rearranged to include service standards, competitive
bidding, and results-based contracting.     MOSA has already developed new service
standards and criteria for the Care Program (largest social program). A Ministerial decree
would be issuedputtingthe new standards ineffect.

(iii) ReformoftheservicesoftheMinistryofHealth(MoH)toimproveefficiencyand
enable M o H to focus its attention on developing healthpolicy and playing its stewardship
role.   Reforms in the short-term will include the implementation o f an enhanced
electronic claims system; the preparation and use o f thirty admission protocols for
expensive procedures and the enforcement o f an administrative cap on the monthly
ceiling amounts for contracts with private hospitals. The enhanced electronic claims
processing system will allow for the implementation o f comprehensive utilization review
procedures to better monitor and control inappropriate health service utilization. Along
with this system, the admission protocols are needed to ensure the appropriateness o f
inpatient coverage, as opposed to referringpatients for outpatient services. The ex ante
application o f caps will help prevent overruns o f the monthly ceiling budgets on the
contracts with private hospitals. With these efficiency-improving measures, the Ministry
o f Health will also work to re-align its budget in coordination with the Ministry o f
Finance towards enhanced spending on public health and primary healthcare services.
This will include defining a public-private strategy that identifies the services to be
directed to public versus private hospitals.

(iv)    Implementation o f the recently completed education sector strategy to enable
Lebanon to capitalize on its most vibrant resource (human) when utilized efficiently and
effectively. Through support from the World Bank, reforms o f the education sector will
continue and will include education finance, re-organization o f the management o f the
public education system (specifically the Ministry o f Education and Higher Education),
and implementation o f a nationally agreed upon education sector strategy to achieve
better outcomes fiom the significant level o f spendingalready taking place.

                      VI.      OPERATION IMPLEMENTATION

A.      POVERTY AND SOCIAL IMPACT

75.     The distributional impacts o f specific policies supported by the proposed
operation are expected to be modest. Indeed actions taken to date that the operation
supports would if anything improve the welfare of the population through more
employment generation through the improved business climate, better targeting o f social
services and improved service reliability from the grid connected electricity network
which in turn would lead to reduced reliance on more costly self-generation (reported to
be about twice the cost for residentialconsumers).

76.     The overall Paris I11 reform program, o f which the proposed operation is
supporting a sub-set, comprises a series o f reform measures which could have significant
distributional impact on consumers in Lebanon, ranging from the (i)       impact tax policy
reforms, including lifting o f the cap on the gasoline excise rate; (ii)     privatization o f
utilityservices, includinginthe first instance telecommunications and later electricity and
water; and (iii)  the curtailing o f expenditures through, inter alia, public wages through
closing of various funds and greater automation o f administrative procedures (which in
itself, on the other hand, may generate more jobs through an increase in business
establishments).

77.     The Bank plans to cany out a Poverty and Social Impact Assessment (PSIA)
starting in September, 2007 to assess the social and poverty impacts o f the key Paris I11
reform agenda, including the areas where the Bank has assumed leadership (business
environment, energy and social). Since the proposed operation has been redesigned as a
single-tranche operation focused mainly on institutional actions, it has not been deemed
requiredfor this first stage.

78.     As reforms deepen, the PSIA will review the distributional impacts o f potential
electricity pricing adjustments, social impacts o f labor retrenchment and include
stakeholder consultations. It is important to note, however, that any future price changes
may not impact the most vulnerable consumers since there i s a significant opportunity to
better target the subsidies and to restructure the tariff blocks with the current block
system being over-generous. On social retrenchment, the average age of staff inEdL i s
59 so many will naturally retire inthe next few years Secondly, as part o fthe Emergency
TA project under the LTF, the advisory support to restructure EdL includes design o f
retrenchment and severance pay schemes.

79.     The Bank's on-going support to the social sectors, as well as any future DPLs
including social sector support, will also review whether measures are needed from a
social perspective to enable successful restructuring o f EdL and to protect vulnerable
consumers from the impacts o f price adjustments. However, it i s important to keep in
mindthat there is a staffing need inEdL so ifanything there will be hiring of new skills,
expertise and talent to underpinthe needfor improved performance. The advisory service
under the Emergency TA project to assist with the restructuring of EdL includes in the


                                             31

scope o f work o f the consultants design o f EdL organizational structure and staffing
needs as a corporatized entity under commercial operation. The net staffing situation o f
EdL will therefore be better known once the consultants have delivered this part of the
work.   There have been extensive consultations throughout, including as part o f the
passing o f Law 462 which had unanimous support inParliament. Furthermore, duringthe
preparation o f the proposed DPL, consultation has continued with different political
groups. Broader consultations will take place as part o fthe PSIA.

80.     The social protection reforms, initiated under the proposed operation, and which
will be implementedover the next 12-15 months are expected to have immediate welfare
impact among the disadvantaged.       For instance, scaling up the Rights and Access
Program for the Disabled and focusing it on the poor disabled population will improve
welfare o f its beneficiaries. Service standards and accreditation o f service providers
within the MOSA Care Program should ensure better quality services.               Overall, an
effective public safety net which i s non-confessional based, a functioning public health
system, and expanded social insurance coverage can contribute to building social
cohesion. With weak and ineffective public institutions and support, the poor have hadto
rely on their respective confessional organizations for income support and other services.
An effective poverty alleviation program with the benefits coming from the state will
send a clear message that the Lebanese state is capable o f fulfilling its core functions, one
o f which i s supporting the poor. Furthermore, the social reforms will play a key role in
mitigating some o fthe macro-critical reforms.




                                            32

B.      ENVIRONMENTAL IMPACT


81.     The operation, which is supporting specific reforms in business environment,
social protection and insurance as well as the energy sector, i s not likely to have any
significant effects on the environment, natural resources or forests. The reforms in the
business environment and social protectiodinsurance are o f administrative and
institutional nature, as are most of the reforms inthe energy sector. The actions to date
are examples o fthis and are based on existing infrastructure.

82.     In the energy sector, however, future reforms related to the overall reform
program will have impacts - positive15 through the increased use of natural gas over gas-
oil and, eventually also possibly, fuel-oil, but also potentially such that need proper
mitigation measures (e.g., infrastructure development arising from the introduction o f
natural gas to additional power plants beyond the Beddawi plant for which infrastructure
already exists and converting the Master Plan - which will include due consideration to
undertakinga sectoral environmentalassessment - and other administrative reforms taken
to date to implemented physical projects). The following mechanisms have therefore
been identified to monitor the potential environmental impact aspects o f the energy
sector:

    the same global perspective covering three interlocking complementary issues: (i)
    investment requirements meeting modern energy needs for developing countries
    while providing attention to efficiency and local environmental considerations; (ii)
    additional steps needed inthe energy sector to address climate change mitigation; and
    (iii)the impactofclimatechangeandtheneedto adequately adapttochangesin
    climate and weather variability. It will also contain specific recommendations to
    reduce emissions o f local (PM, SOX, etc) and global (C02) air pollutants through
    adequate selection o f fuel and generation technologies;

     C.     FIDUCIARY ASPECTS




                                                  34

84.      The Public Financial Management (PFM) system in Lebanon is in a transition
phase and the Government o f Lebanon continues to be committed to reformingthe PFM
system. Significant progresshastaken place inthe PFMsystem over the last few years.

85.      The Country Financial Accountability Assessment (CFAA16), which was
completed inMay 2005, concluded that the overall risk assessment o f the PFM system in
Lebanon i s significant. This overall risk assessmenttakes into account the recent reforms
to the PFM system, including the (i)          computerization o f the budget reporting system; (ii)
consolidation o f all treasury operations at the Ministry o f Finance (MOF) with plans to
move towards the use o f a Single Treasury Account, (iii)generation o f periodical
statistical reports; and (iv) issuance o f payments to public sector employees through
transfers to bank accounts to increase the control aspects o f the payment process.

86.      The CFAA report includes recommendations to decrease the overall fiduciary risk
from significant to moderate. The recommendations include: (i)              increasing the coverage
o f the budget to reflect capital investment financed by donors and extra budgetary
en ti tie^'^; (ii)establishing a modern internal audit function initially within the MOF; and
(iii)reformingtheCourtofAccountsthroughmakingitindependentoftheGovernment
with a focus on expressing an opinion on the fairness o f the Government's annual
financial statements and introducing performance audit functions to its operations, and
moving gradually from the current cash basis accounting system to the one using the
accrual principles.

87.      To implement the outstanding reform measures to the PFM system, the M O F has
appointed a Reform Team dedicatedto the monitoring o f the implementation o f the PFM
reform agenda. The team's assignment started after the Paris I11Donor Conference with
focus on the 2008 budget preparation process and better coordination o f the activities o f
the various agencies aimed at improving the budget allocation. This is being conducted
under a medium-term expenditure framework that provides ceilings on spending by
agencies. In accordance with the Government's reform program presented to the Donor
Conference, the revised budget preparation process initiated the introduction o f a "top
down" approach to budget preparation, extended the budget coverage to includetreasury
transfers and foreign financed capital projects'*.             Indeed, the 2008 budget circular
incorporated the medium term fiscal framework prepared by the Ministryo f Finance to
serve as a guideline for spendingagencies inpreparingtheir budget proposals, with strict
instructions to cut down spending and respect the global budget ceiling set for 2008.
Also the budget circular introduced the reforms planned by the Government for budget
preparation during the coming period, and the prerequisite actions to be taken by

spending agencies.          The Reform Team i s also in charge o f reforming cash flow

88.      The recently signed EPCA with the IMF gives strong weight to the PFM reform
agenda through identifying three PFM reform outputs to be achieved by September
200719. Implementingthese reforms will lead to a comprehensive budgetthat includes all
capital projects, an improved control environment andmore transparent entities operating
outside o fthe Government.

89.      Inaddition to the PFM actions included inthe EPCA agreement, the MOF is in

covering the short, medium and long term.

90.      The reforms are supported by a detailed time bound action plan, presentedat the
Paris I11conference. This planwill be usedas the benchmark for monitoring progress o f
the reforms that will lead to stronger financial accountability and better transparency in
the use o f public funds. Progress inthese areas could lead to a reduced fiduciary risk to
"moderate" level inthe horizon o f2008-2009.

loan, against satisfactory completion of the agreed upon actions and declared
effectiveness o fthe Loan.

Borrower has a treasury account (number 36) usedto finance general budget expenditures
at the Central Bank o f Lebanon (BDL). The proposed operation's loan amount would be
disbursed into this treasury account.          The Government has determined that the net
proceeds from the Loan will be utilized for the replacement or refinancing o f existing
