         ANNEX 3-BACKGROUND TO THE LEBANESEELECTRICITY SECTOR


1. SECTOR STRUCTURE

The primarylegal framework for Lebanon's electricity sector is currently articulated inDecree Law
6919 (14/12/2001), which mainly spells out the role o f Electricit6 du Liban (EdL), the national
electricity utility, andLaw 462 (02/09/2002), which defines the roles o f the Government, the various
entities in the sector, and that o f the private sector, as well as the structure o f the sector, and the
principles o fprivate sector involvement.

The Council of Ministers and the Ministry of Energy & Water

Key decisions on Lebanon's strategic energy issues are made at the Council o f Ministers level, but
uponsubmission of proposals on specific projects or electricity sector draft laws and decrees by the
Minister o f Energy and Water (MOEW).            The latter is responsible for the petroleum sector,
electricity and water sectors and i s organized around three main Directorate Generals (namely,
Exploitation, Hydraulic and Electricity Resources as well as Petroleum).

The MOEW is primarily responsible for the formulation of energy sector policy, regulation, sector
development planning, and electricity and petroleum product price determination. Pricing i s also
subject to approval by the Council o fMinisters. The MOEW has also beenmandated to import fuel
oil and diesel onbehalf o f EdL.

Lebanon Oil Installations

Lebanon Oil Installations (LOI), a state-owned company, imports gas oil and fuel oil that i s sold to
local distributors. After 1987, in addition to the LOI, private companies were also allowed to
import petroleum products (for example Wardieh Holding Inc., Total Liban, Coral Oil Company,
etc.).   LO1 i s also in charge o f the construction, management, operation and maintenance o f
petroleum product installations. It works as a commercial entity and controls storage facilities,
research laboratories and two refineries (Beddawi and Zahrani). These two refineries, however,
ceased operation in 1989 and 1992, respectively, and are now used only as import terminals and
storage facilities for refinedoil products.

LO1 owns the Lebanese share o f the Syria-Lebanon gas pipeline (GASYLE), up to Homs in Syria
and the adjacent land. LO1also built a natural gas station at its Tripoli branch, inorder to receive
the gasthat would come from Syria andeventually supplythe Beddawipower plant.

Diesel for transportation i s imported directly by distributors. MOEW issues licenses for import and
sale o f gasoline and other petroleumproducts.

The LPG market is dominated by a private monopoly. The Government is responsible for LPG
marketregulationbycontrolling purchasesthrough a quota systemandLPGpricing.

Prime Minister's OfJice


                                                  134

Given the gravity of the power sector in Lebanon, its dramatic impact on the country's economy,
people's basic needs, and Government's budget, and given the urgency to reverse the negative trend
o f the sector, the Prime Minister's Office i s directly involved inensuringthat the Government sector
reform program be quickly finalized, endorsed, and, more importantly implemented. To ensure
close oversight o f the reforms and the key associated decisions, the Prime Minister chairs an Inter-
Ministerial Reform Committee for the sector which meets regularly.

Ministry of Finance

Given the substantial fiscal burden o f the energy sector on Lebanon's budget, and the regular and
need for the Government to subsidize, through means o f transfers, the national utility, the Ministry
o f Finance also monitors the finances and applies strict oversight on the sector. As a key member o f
the high-level inter-ministerial committee on energy, it has been ensuring that prompt and subsidy
reducing reform measuresare prioritized underthe reform program.

Electricite` du Liban

EdL is a state-owned electricity utility created in 1964. It currently holds a monopoly on electricity
generation, transmission and distribution, with the exception o f three private concessions operating
inhydro-generation andarrangements withfour private concessions onthe distribution side.

EdL is expected to participate in the formulation o f the electricity sector policy and the design of
electricity projects. Its official mandate calls for protecting national priorities and the environment,
meetingregionaldevelopment goals, promoting competition and safe-guarding consumer interests.
EdL's daily operational decisions are made by its management, while more strategic decisions are
made by a seven-member Board o f Directors, appointed, along with the Director General, by the
Council o f Ministers.      EdL counts 2,300 staff today, far below what a utility operating a
comparative system should count. Furthermore, with its personnel having an average age o f 57
years, EdLhas been experiencing a steady decrease instaff, due for retirement. Unfortunately, EdL,
as all state-owned enterprises in Lebanon, i s currently under a national hiring fkeeze, making it
particularly challenging to replace and strengthenan already-weak work force, as it can only rely on
limitedcontracting to fillpart o fits wideninghumanresource gap.

Councilfor Reconstruction and Development

Over the past 17years, the Council for Development and Reconstruction (CDR), which had initially
been set up to replace the Ministry o f Planning to streamline and speed up the approval and
financing o f reconstruction projects, plays a key role in the planning and financing o f electricity
infrastructure projects.

2. MAINCHALLENGES AND ISSUES

The sector faces several challenges, among them:

Reliance on importedfuel for power generation


                                                    135

Enjoyingvery little natural resources o f its own, Lebanon is obliged to import most o f its energy to
meet its electricity needs, only marginally complemented by in-country hydro-generation and
biomass production. Petroleum products constitute 96% o f the primary energy imported and coal
the remaining4%.

Lebanon's total installed capacity i s estimated at about 2,243 Megawatt (MW) with thermal
generation providing the bulk o f the electricity supply (close to 90%) and the remaining balance
contributed by hydro-plants. Around 120 MW o f capacity i s ensured though imports. The main
issue in the generation o f power i s the reliance on gas-oil in the absence o f natural gas in the two
combined-cycle plants which account for about 50% o f installed capacity. This comes at a cost o f
approximately US$700/ton instead o f US$350/ton. Recent estimates show that substantial savings
could be realized, should there be a switch to natural gas. Indeed, at today's oil prices, annual
savings from fuel switchingcould reach close to US$400 million.24.

More generally, all of the power plants suffer from shortage in spare parts, lack o f maintenance,
unskilledoperators, and investmentfor modernization.

Lebanon's hydro potential consists of three hydro stations operated by state-owned local offices,
namelyKadisha, Bared, and Litani, andtwo stations operated by concessions, namelyNahr Ibrahim
and Safa. The total hydro capacity i s about 280 MW and supplied over 1,000 GWh to the grid in
2005, though seasonal productionvaries greatly dependingon rainfalls.

Since 1995, a small portion o f Lebanon's electricity supply has also been met through electricity
imports from Syria through the 66 kV lines in the Center-East and a 220 kV line in the North o f
Lebanon. The total import capacity i s equivalent to around 120 MW.

A Demand-SupplyGap

Today, recorded electricity supply i s insufficient to meet demand. Since the end o f the 1975-1990
civil war, EdLhas operated at full capacity with no reserve margin(international standard i s 10%to
15%). Power cuts have beenendemic with several commercial and industrialconsumers reporting
200 cuts per year. The power suppliedby EdLreflected inhistorical statistics therefore only reflects
EDL'scapacity to supply electricity, andnot the actual demand.

The average load factor o f the Lebanese system was 46% in 2006, with a load factor o f 40% for
residential consumers and 50% for industries. The corresponding peak demand for EdL in 2006
was 1,984 MW andthe total system peak demand i s estimated as 2,316 MW, including 332 MW o f
self-generation in the same year. However, the capacity of self-generator may be higher, as the
proposed estimate i s based on the assumption that the load factor o f self-generators i s the same as
for E&. The recent Investment Climate Assessment (ICA) indicated that industries use their self-
generation capacity during the peak hours (four hours daily) due to relatively better price, and rely
on EdLfor the remainder o f the day.

The extent o f self-generation is difficult to evaluate and is not recorded. However, recent studies
and investigations provide a basis for estimating the demand served through self-generation.

24Assumingthat piped natural gas wouldbe introducedat Beddawipower plantbymid2008 and LNG at Zahrani power
plant by mid2009.

                                                     136

According to the 2004 study prepared by Chubu Electric Power Company, as part of the
Hydrocarbon Strategy Study, about 80% of industrial users and 75% of commercial buildings have
their own self-generation capacity. The ICA indicated that 100% of large and medium-sized
entrepreneurs surveyed declared they have their own generators. As they do not rely exclusively on
their own generators to meet their needs, but call on EdL during the low tariff period, it i s estimated
that large and medium industries with high voltage connections rely for about 40% of the time on
fuel oil fired self-generation, and 60% of the time on EdL. For low voltage consumers, the estimate
i s that small diesel generators meet about 10% of their demand. The resulting estimate i s that -
overall - self-generationmeets about 20% ofthe demand.

Poor networkperformance

The transmission network i s fraught with problems. The main 220kV backbone grid i s not yet fully
completed, nor is the long-planned national load control center in place (although a contract has
been awarded for this). Major investments are also required in the medium- and low-voltage
transmission and distribution systems as well as inmetering to reduce transmission and distribution
losses. But EdL's worsening deficiency in managerial and technical capacity, as well as poor
planning and execution of funding have long delayedimplementation of investment programs.

Technical losses are estimated at about 15%, while another 17.8% loss i s explained by illegal
connections. The billing information system is reported to be outdated and poorly maintained
resulting inhigh degree of errors and inaccuracy in monitoring billing and collections performance
(see below).

A substantial share of the electricity demandmet by EDL is not billed

The widening gap between generationand billing was further aggravated in 2006 due to the impact
ofthe July 2006 hostilities.

 ource: EDL Billing &Production data

Unbilledelectricity is not recorded and can, therefore, only be estimated as the difference between
recorded generated electricity and billed electricity, minus the technical losses. Assuming that
generation and import data are accurate, overall losses in2005 were evaluated at 3,372 GWh (33%
o f generated electricity). According to EdL, technical losses represent 15% o f electricity produced
or 22% o f electricity billed, and by difference, power thefts represent 17.8% o f generated electricity
(27% o f electricity billed).

Electricity tar@ are high by regional standards but too low to cover cost

Lebanon's electricity tariff level i s highby regional standards. The overall average tariff for 2006,
based on billed energy, i s LL 1 4 l k W h(US$cents 9.4 kwh).Compared to electricity prices inthe
region, the Lebanese tariff level falls in the upper bracket. Indeed, average tariff in Morocco (9),
Jordan (7), Tunisia (4.6), or Egypt (2.8) i s equal or belowLebanon's average tariff.

Inthe region, Lebanonpresents more similarities with countries largelydepending on imported fuel
for power generation, such as Morocco and Jordan, and the Lebanese tariff level i s comparable to
these countries. The fuel import structure o f the comparable countries, however, i s rather different.
InMorocco, power generation is dependenton imported coal, HFO, and to a lesser extent, on gas,
but not diesel, while Jordan does not import any diesel fuel. In contrast, Lebanon is in a unique
situation for a non-landlocked country to depend on diesel for half o f its fuel (1.10 million tons of
diesel against 1.O milliontons o f HFO in2006), with a considerable cost penalty.

According to the sector regulation, the EdL tariff i s set and approved by MOEW, based on EdL's
recommendation. Inpractice, however, it has been set by the Council o f Minister. The tariff i s not
based on the cost o f supply, and does not reflect the system's long run marginal cost. The 2006
tariff structure is summarizedbelow.

Opportunities exist to improve the tariff structure.      The residential tariff subsidizes the lower
tranches o f consumption, but the tranches are wide and the invertedblock tariff should peak at about
300 kWh, as is the case inmost countries, instead o f the 500 kWhthresholds at present; consumers
with a consumptiono felectricity inexcess of 300 kWhmay not qualify as economically vulnerable.

The tariff for highand medium-voltage supply to the concessions and few other customers seems to
allow for an excessive distribution margin or high voltage discount, typically in the range o f US$
cents 1.5 to 2 kWh, with a considerable loss o f revenue for EdL. .

The poor tariff structure encourages self-generation in industry. The average tariff in industry is
US$ cents 10kWh on average (US$cents 20kWh at peak hours) and the peak tariff for industry i s
below the cost o f self generation using HFO, which encourages industries to operate their own
generator and to reduce their recourse to EdL. The existing tariff structure therefore makes it
financially attractive for large and medium size industriesto shift to self-generation duringpeak and
intermediate demand. For large residential consumers, once they have purchased a generator to
protect against power supply un-reliability, the cost o frunningtheir generator i s competitive against
the EdL tariff.


                                                   139

EdL's management systems are outdatedand inefficient

Accounting and financial reporting systems are not functioning adequately and do not easily allow
for the annual closing o f EdL's accounts and their auditing. The consumer management system i s
not reliable while the humanresources management system i s also inefficient.

The serious technical deficiencies in the sector are closely related to the poor governance o f the
sector and the lack o f implementation o f much needed reforms o f EdL. Indeed, a major problem is
the legal status of EdL as a non-commercial "EtablissementPubZic", which puts EdL's staff under
the status o f civil servants, with an inadequate pay scale to retain competent managers and technical
staff and career management rules incompatible with the operation o f a utility. EdL's staff i s about
57 years old on average and EdL i s currently losing around 150 staff per year to retirementout o f its
2,300 current employees. This is further aggravated by the lack o f a long term investment plan to
orient investment decisions based on economic and technical criteria and to ensure that investment
decisions are made in a timely manner. Finally, the vicious circle o f lack of financing due to the
socio-political difficulty to charge cost reflective tariffs, leading to lack o f investment and poor
service, which in turn encourages power thefts and poor payment discipline, results in a widening
financial gap. This gap i s made that much more vulnerable to changes in international oil prices as
Lebanon imports all o f its energy needs.




                                                   140

The electricity demand used for the base year (2006) was calculated as EDL's billed electricity reported for
2006, majored by electricity thefts (17.8%) and decreased by the estimated level of self-generation by class of
customers.

2) Fuel Prices: all fuel prices were kept constant, using US$ 65.9/bbl as reference (US$64/bbl plus 3% to
reflect Lebanon's specific contractual terms with oil suppliers). US$64/bbl is based on the IMF's forecast
price for crude oil (and also the 2006 average price of a barrel of crude oil recorded by the World Bank for
Dubai, Brent i3 WTI).

2.I.Diesel price.

Diesel was priced at US$ 613.06/ton, based on the above price of crude oil per barrel and an aggregated
conversion ratio of 9.3, calculated as detailed below.

2.I.I.For Convertina Crude Oil price Der barrel to Gasoil (Diesel)DriceDer ton

    (a) Conversion of Crude Oil price Der barrel to Diesel Drice Der barrel

         The conversion was calculated as follows: Crude Oil price/bbl X 1.24 = Diesel pricelbbl with 1.24
         being the average price difference between these two fuel types in 2006 on the Mediterranean oil
         market.

    (b) Conversionof Diesel mice Der barrel to Diesel urice Der ton

         The conversion was calculated as follows: Diesel price/bbl X 7.52 = Diesel pricelton

         With 7.52 being the fixed unit conversion factor for Diesel.

    (c) The resultina aaareaated conversion factor

        An aggregated factor of 9.3 reflecting the two consecutive conversions described above was
        obtained as follows:

        Crude Oil pricelbbl X 1.24X 7.52 = Crude Oil pricelbbl X 9.3 = Diesel price/ton

2.2.Heavy Fuel Oil I% Sulfur Content (HFO I%)

Heavy fuel oil 1% was priced at US$ 3ll/ton, based on the above price of crude oil per barrel and an
aggregated conversion ratio of 4.72, calculated as detailed below.

2.2.1.For Convertina Crude Oil Drice Der barrel to Heavv Fuel Oil 1% (HF0 I%) mice Der ton

    (d) Conversion of Crude Oil Drice Der barrel to HFO 1% mice Der barrel

         The conversion was calculated as follows: Crude Oil price/bbl X 0.71 = HFO 1% price/bbl with 0.71
         being the average price difference between these two fuel types in 2006 on the Mediterranean oil
         market.

    (e) Conversion of HFO 1% Drice Der barrel to HFO 1% mice Der ton

         The conversion was calculated as follows: HFO 1% price/bbl X 6.67 = HFO 1% pricelton

         With 6.67 being the fixed unit conversion factor for HFO 1%

    (9 The resultina aaareaatedconversion factor

         An aggregated factor of 4.72 reflecting the two consecutive conversions described above was
         obtained as follows:

         Crude Oil price/bblX 0.71 X 6.67 = Crude Oil price/bbl X 4.72 = HFO 1% price/ton

Note:The fact that the price of HFO 1% is inferior to the Crude Oil price is mainly driven by a market where
more refined, environmentally-friendly and energy efficient fuels (such as Diesel for example) are in higher
demand than heavier fuels, which are in excess supply on the market. As a result, the cost of crude oil is
more than recovered by refining companies by the sale price of refined fuels, allowing them to sell heavier
fuels at lower price than the Crude Oil price.

2.3. Natural Gas

Natural gas was priced at 5.65$/mmbtu (piped to the plant) and Il$/mmbtu (LNG to plant). Details of the
calculation described below.

3) Total Fuel Costs: fuel cost projections from 2007 on have been calculated as the total volume (in tons) of
HFO 1% and Diesel reportedly consumed in 2006 by EDL (inflated to reflect the annual rate of demand
growth above), multiplied by the respectivefuel prices per ton.




Actions IrnDactina Revenues


                                                      142

I)Improved billing: calculatesthe savings derived from improvements in billingthrough the incremental
implementation of 3 theft reduction programs, as proposed by CRA, reducing annual theft by 6.7% in 2008 up
to 60% by 2012. This is equivalent to bringing EDL's 2007 theft level (as % of total supplied electricity) from
18% to 8% by 2012. The calculation of billing has also factored the annual growth in demand.

Actions Impactina Expenses

1) Fuel Conversion of Beddawi to Gas: calculates the savings from switching the Beddawi plant supply
from diesel to Egyptian piped natural gas as of July 1, 2008.

The unit price of piped gas (US$ 5.65/mmbtu) was calculated based on an agreed unit price of US$
5.00/mmbtu under the recent agreement with Egypt and an additional US$ 0.65/mmbtu for transit through
Syria.

The annual savings from the fuel conversion at Beddawi plant are calculated as the annual reduction in fuel
cost.

2) Transmission and Distribution Network Optimization: the reduction in technical losses, derived from a
more efficient electricity dispatch in the electric grid and the associated improved distribution of flows and
loads among sub-stations and network lines, and the improvements to the distribution from private
management which is under tender by the recruited consulting firm CRA, leads to reduced volume of energy
required to meet a given demand.           Therefore, the savings from the optimization of EDL's system
management and its associated drop in technical losses from 15% in 2006 to 10% by 2012 were calculated
as the share of total fuel cost that could be reduced annually.

3) Power Purchase: this is the cost of purchasing power from small local private producers (mostly hydro)
and from Syria (120 MW), using 2006 levels as a baseline and assuming an annual average cost growth rate
of 2%.

4) Wages: this is the total cost of EDL's wages, assuming IO%, 10% and 8% increases in wage costs over
2008, 2009 and 2010 respectively,due to the corporatization and restaffing of EDL.

5) O&M: this is the cost of works and services as well as transports, using 2006 as a baseline and assuming
an annual average cost growth rate of 2%.

6) Other: these expenses cover Board expenses, various taxes, and general O&M expenses, using 2006 as
a baseline and assuming an annual average cost growth rate of 2%.




                                                       143

Summary of Assumptions for the Additional Measures

1) Fuel Conversion of Zahrani to LNG: the potential additional savings obtained from switching the Zahrani
plant supply from diesel to LNG as of January 1, 2010, were estimated as detailed below.

The unit price of LNG (US$ Illmbtu) was based on the LNG price the Lebanese Government expected to
get in any future negotiated contract for LNG supply.

The annual savings from the fuel conversion at Zahrani plant are calculated as the annual reduction in fuel
cost, in the same way as itwas the case of Beddawi, but decreased by:

    LNG Terminal investment cost, estimated at US160 million, based on international experience, and
    amortized over 15 years. The international experience refers to Italy, Pakistan, and California, where
    larger floating facilities were used for an average investment cost of US$150 million. Investment cost
    includes a floating storage and re-gasification unit (FSRU);
    An operating costs calculated as 3.3% of the total investment cost, based on international experience;
0   US$ 1.30/mbtu for transport costs.

2) Fuel Monitoring: the potential additional savings derived from a stricter fuel chain monitoring (of both
heavy fuel oil and diesel) were estimated based on a 5% annual improvement in EDL's overall fuel
consumption, minus an estimated US$ 1 million/year for surveillance costs, based on international
experience.

3) Revision of Heavy Fuel Oil specs: the potential additional savings derived from switching heavy fuel oil
(HFO) standard from 1% to 3.5% sulfur content were calculated assuming a 4% annual HFO cost saving,
less the annual cost of environmental mitigation measures through Flue Gas Desulphurization (FGD)
investments (estimated at US$ 40 million). EDL is currently supplied with the 1% sulfur content HFO from the
Black Sea market (the only available market) while its choice for supply of 3.5% sulfur content HFO is much
wider and, therefore, prices are likely to be more competitive. The 4% cost saving is derived from
international experience.
